        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";
        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/adfm.202414627')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'd6c4d6b2-9e85-4a0c-aa0b-8b0812765789'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d137282488" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The O3-type NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NM) layered cathode in sodium ion batteries (SIBs) undergoes structural distortion and capacity degradation during cycling, which seriously hinders its practical application. Herein, lanthanum (La) is employed as a dopant in O3-NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5-x</sub>La<sub>x</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NML) cathodes, which triggered an “atomic knife” effect, reducing particle size, and stabilizing crystal structure. The larger La ions generated structural strain during grain growth at high temperatures, hindering the movement of grain boundaries and refining the size of NML particles. Comprehensive characterizations illuminated La doping-induced atomic site occupancy and phase transformations within NML. A competitive phase formation between layered NML and perovskite LaMnO<sub>3</sub> (LMO) is observed. Spontaneously formed perovskite LMO provides surface protection. Moreover, strong La─O bonds expand the Na interlayer spacing, enhancing Na<sup>+</sup>-ion diffusion. Consequently, NML cathodes exhibit superior long-term cycling stability and ultrahigh rate capacities compared to pristine NM cathode and most currently reported layered cathodes for SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202414627-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202414627-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have garnered the most success in energy storage fields, which has dominated the power markets for portable electronic devices, electric vehicles and new energy generation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0001_R_d137282480e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0005_R_d137282480e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Mater. Rep.: Energy">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, due to the resource and cost limitations, LIBs can hardly meet the growing demand of electrochemical energy storage. Therefore, alternative new battery systems with more abundant resources and comparable performances are encouraging, among of which, sodium ion batteries (SIBs) is considered as one of the most attractive energy storage devices due to their abundant raw resources of sodium and similar operation mechanisms with LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0006_R_d137282480e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a>-<a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0011_R_d137282480e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Carbon Neutralization">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The cathode materials play a critical role in SIBs systems by providing free Na<sup>+</sup>-ions and determining battery potentials. Among various reported cathode candidates, layered transition metal (TM) oxides are widely considered as promising candidates for practical SIBs cathodes due to their facile fabrication process, high theoretical capacity, and similar energy storage mechanism as that of the layered oxide cathodes for commercial LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0012_R_d137282480e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a>-<a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0014_R_d137282480e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Stor. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Generally, the mainstream studied structures of the layered oxide cathodes are P2-type and O3-type.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0015_R_d137282480e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">15</a>-<a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0018_R_d137282480e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compare to the P2-type oxides with insufficient Na<sup>+</sup>, O3-type oxides have full Na<sup>+</sup> in their crystalline structure, making them more preferable when applied in full cells.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0015_R_d137282480e540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Specifically, the O3-type NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, benefiting from stable Mn<sup>4+</sup>-O framework and high theoretical capacity by two electron transfer of Ni<sup>2+</sup>↔Ni<sup>4+</sup>, becomes one of the most promising candidates.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0017_R_d137282480e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the obvious multiple phase transitions (O3<sub>hex.</sub>-O3′<sub>mon.</sub>-P3<sub>hex.</sub>-P′3<sub>mon.</sub>-P3′<sub>hex.</sub>-O3′<sub>hex.</sub>) make the O3-NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode suffers from serious structural distortion during Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion process, which induces grain crack, voltage hysteresis, and capacity fade.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0019_R_d137282480e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Stor. Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, another nonnegligible problem for the sodium-based layered oxide cathodes, especially O3-type cathodes, is their surface moisture instability. When the materials are placed in the humid air, the sodium-ions tend to extract from the lattice structure, and form NaOH and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> on the surfaces of particles, which will increase the interfacial resistance and deteriorate electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0020_R_d137282480e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Therefore, exploring feasible strategies to suppress the multiple phase transitions and unwanted surface side reactions of the O3-NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode is significant for promoting the practical application of such materials. Currently, lattice doping by various electroactive elements (e.g., Co,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0021_R_d137282480e608" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Fe,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0022_R_d137282480e614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a>, <a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0023_R_d137282480e614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Cu,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0022_R_d137282480e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a>, <a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0024_R_d137282480e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> etc.) and electroinactive elements (e.g., Ti,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0022_R_d137282480e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a>-<a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0024_R_d137282480e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Mg,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0025_R_d137282480e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Mater. Chem. Front.">25</a>, <a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0026_R_d137282480e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Zn,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0027_R_d137282480e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Alloys Compd.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Al,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0028_R_d137282480e640" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Phys. Chem. C">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sn,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0029_R_d137282480e646" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sb,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0030_R_d137282480e651" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ACS Nano">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nb,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0031_R_d137282480e656" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Eng. J.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> etc.) as well as surface decorations (e.g., Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0032_R_d137282480e666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Ceram. Int.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> ZrO<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0033_R_d137282480e674" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Energy Stor. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> CuO,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0034_R_d137282480e679" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NaPO<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0035_R_d137282480e686" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> K<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0036_R_d137282480e695" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nano Res.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> etc.) have been proved to benefit their structural stability upon cycling and environmental stability in storage. In our previous study, we demonstrated the significant reduction in particle size by doping Sb ions in O3-type NM cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0030_R_d137282480e700" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ACS Nano">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This phenomenon of particle size reduction by element doping has also been reported from a study on Sn-doped O3-NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5-x</sub>Sn<sub>x</sub>O<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0037_R_d137282480e715" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ACS Nano">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These observations support the idea that some specific ion doping can inhibit grain growth of layered oxide materials. The reduction in particle size brings two advantages: first, it shortens the diffusion distance of Na<sup>+</sup>-ions; second, it mitigates significant lattice strain during the insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>-ions into/from the crystal structure.</p>
         
         <p>However, this phenomenon of reducing particle size through doping is not common in all doped layered oxides, for instance, doping with Zn,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0038_R_d137282480e727" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ACS Nano">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Cu,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0039_R_d137282480e732" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Energy Chem.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Co<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0040_R_d137282480e737" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Zr<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0041_R_d137282480e743" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Nano Energy">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> etc. did not show such effect. Therefore, we propose a hypothesis that the “atomic knife” effect observed in layered oxide materials when doped with specific atoms may be attributed to variations in ionic radius. For instance, the ion radius of Sb<sup>3+</sup> measures at 0.76 Å, while the ion radii of Ni<sup>2+</sup>/<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup>/<sup>4+</sup> are 0.69 Å /0.60 Å and 0.64 Å /0.53 Å, respectively. To validate this hypothesis, our ongoing research focuses on investigating the influence of doped atoms with larger ionic radius on O3-NM materials, with a particular highlight on their cutting effect. This investigation has prompted us to propose a novel technique known as the “atomic knife”.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, La<sup>3+</sup> ions were adopted as the appropriate dopant candidate owing to its comparatively larger ionic radius of 1.03 Å. Subsequently, a series of O3-NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5-x</sub>La<sub>x</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (x = 0, 0.01, 0.02, and 0.03) materials (labeled as NM, NML1, NML2, and NML3) were synthesized by a citrate sol-gel method. The morphology characterizations illustrate that a significant grain refinement is manifested in La-doped materials. The Rietveld refinements of X-ray diffraction (XRD), high resolution transmission electron microscope (HRTEM), and density functional theory (DFT) calculations of the formation energies further prove that La element is enriched on the surface of grains and forms a stable perovskite-like layer, protecting the materials from moisture and electrolyte damages. A reasonable hypothesis is proposed in this work, which is the suppression of grain boundary line movement during the crystallization process by the doped La<sup>3+</sup>-ions with much larger radius. As a result, high capacity, excellent rate and cycle performances are obtained by NML2 sample, which reaches a high reversible capacity of 197.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C in 2–4.2&nbsp;V, and maintains 96.9% over 100 cycles at 1 C in a potential range of 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V. This performance far surpasses what has been reported in the existing literature. The in situ XRD further demonstrates that the NML2 sample exhibits fewer phase transitions, less lattice change, and higher reversibility during the (de)sodiation process. This research has introduced innovative concepts for the future design of layered oxide cathode materials for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202414627-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202414627-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The O3-NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5-x</sub>La<sub>x</sub>O<sub>2</sub> materials were prepared by a citrate sol-gel technique according to the procedure shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0001">1a</a>. The sol-gel method can achieve molecular-level uniformity by dispersing raw materials in a low-viscosity solvent. This facilitates rapid and quantitative incorporation of dopant elements during the solution reaction, ensuring thorough mixing at the molecular level in the resulting gel. In the subsequent thermal treatment, the NM and NML products underwent a two-step calcination process. Initially, heating at 500 °C for 5&nbsp;h eliminated residual organic matter and enhanced the crystallization quality. Subsequently, the material was calcined at 900 °C for 12&nbsp;h to promote sufficient growth of NM and NML particles. This sequence of operations is to guarantee the even incorporation of La and to ensure the prominent segmentation effect of the “atomic knife” on flake NML particles. Based on the inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectroscopy (ICP-OES) results, the measured element composition proportions of all prepared samples are very close to their target stoichiometry (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414627-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ad276785-495a-44c0-83f3-7bb5be11c1e2/adfm202414627-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ad276785-495a-44c0-83f3-7bb5be11c1e2/adfm202414627-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b8c2f62c-29ec-4671-9e4c-33fce404e40a/adfm202414627-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ad276785-495a-44c0-83f3-7bb5be11c1e2/adfm202414627-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414627-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414627" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Illustration of the citrate sol-gel synthesis process of O3-NML materials; SEM images of b) NM, c) NML1, d) NML2, and e) NML3 cathode materials (insets: particle size distribution).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0001">1b–e</a> illustrates the morphologies of the prepared NM and NML materials. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0001">1b</a>, the pristine NM sample exhibits uniform flaky morphology with particle width ranging from 1 to 2&nbsp;µm and a thickness of ≈300&nbsp;nm. However, after La-doping, the NML materials show gradually reduced average particle sizes from several micrometers to hundred nanometers with increase of La-doping amount (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0001">1c–e</a>). The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface areas of NM, NML1, NML2, and NML3, as obtained from N<sub>2</sub> adsorption measurements, are 0.69, 1.07, 1.58, and 2.05 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The increase of specific surface areas should be attributed to the decrease in particle size caused by La-doping. From the nitrogen adsorption-desorption isotherms shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), the NM and NML samples possess mesoporous structure with an average pore diameter of ≈10&nbsp;nm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), facilitating for efficient charge transfer.</p>
         
         <p>Notably, decreased particle sizes result in shorter diffusion pathways for Na<sup>+</sup>-ions and reduced stress from structural distortion, thus potentially optimizing the rate and cycle performances. This phenomenon can be attributed to the hindrance of grain boundary movement during high-temperature grain growth caused by barrier of doped larger atoms. The mechanism is schematically shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0002">2</a>, where the large radius of La<sup>3+</sup> causes compressive strain on the TMO<sub>6</sub> layer. During the high-temperature crystallization process, the presence of La atoms tends to promote the formation of grain boundaries, thereby impeding further grain growth. If this mechanism is valid, the majority of La elements are expected to aggregate into alternative structural phases at the edge of the particles. Therefore, XRD and TEM were further conducted to deeply investigate the structures and micromorphologies of the NM and NML materials.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414627-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4b09faef-4a36-4d51-a25d-01e5f75bc63d/adfm202414627-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4b09faef-4a36-4d51-a25d-01e5f75bc63d/adfm202414627-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ff7d2b0c-8d4d-4aeb-b574-2956353aedae/adfm202414627-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4b09faef-4a36-4d51-a25d-01e5f75bc63d/adfm202414627-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414627-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414627" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic diagram of the “atomic knife” mechanism of large atom in inhibiting grain growth.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The phases of the synthesized NM and NML materials are confirmed by XRD. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0003">3a</a>, all the samples possess the hexagonal O3-type <i>α</i>-NaFeO<sub>2</sub> structure with a space group of R-3m.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0042_R_d137282480e902" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Mater. Chem. A">42</a>, <a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0043_R_d137282480e902" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With increasing of La-doping amount, the diffraction peaks of (003) and (104) planes gradually shift toward lower Bragg angles, indicating La-doping-induced d-spacing expansion, and La-ion incorporation into the NML lattice structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0044_R_d137282480e908" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - J. Energy Chem.">44</a>, <a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0045_R_d137282480e908" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Electrochim. Acta.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, it is noteworthy that when the doping amount of La surpasses 2%, an additional perovskite-like LaMnO<sub>3</sub> (LMO) phase with a space group of R-3c is detectable by XRD (the diffraction peak circled in red in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0003">3a</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0046_R_d137282480e919" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414627-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cc536861-7c47-4a04-923d-c24670e7a24f/adfm202414627-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/cc536861-7c47-4a04-923d-c24670e7a24f/adfm202414627-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1046862b-0124-4e98-b3eb-09fd0d6849f7/adfm202414627-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cc536861-7c47-4a04-923d-c24670e7a24f/adfm202414627-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414627-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414627" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structures of O3-NM and NML materials: a) XRD patterns and local enlarged images of (003) and (104) diffraction peaks; b–e) Rietveld refinement patterns of XRD data; f) determined a- and c-axis and lattice volume parameters from XRD refinement; g) schematic diagram of expansion of interlayer spacing by La-doping; h) the formation energies of the layered O3-NML and perovskite LMO phases from DFT calculation.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To obtain quantitative crystallographic information, Rietveld refinements of XRD have been performed. The lattice parameters determined from the refined crystallographic data for all samples (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0003">3b–e</a>) are listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The refined factors (Rwp%) are all less than 10%, implying the reliability and accuracy of the refinement results. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0003">3g</a> schematically illustrates the evolution of lattice parameter by La-doping. Upon La-doping, the lattice parameters of a, c, and the cell volume gradually increase (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0003">3f</a>). possibly due to the larger ionic radius of La<sup>3+</sup> and its stronger electronegativity. The specific coordination environment between TM-O and TM-TM, with or without La-doping, will be further investigated through XAS characterization.</p>
         
         <p>In addition, the amounts of the second phase perovskite-like LMO in NML2 and NML3 samples, expressed as molar ratios, are ≈1.06 and 2.10 mol%, respectively. The simultaneous formation of a second perovskite phase in a layered structure is an intriguing phenomenon, which was also observed by Wang et&nbsp;al. in their study on La-doping of Li<sup>+</sup>-based layered oxide materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0047_R_d137282480e964" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Nature">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To gain insights into the formation conditions of the second phase, DFT calculations were employed to determine the formation energy of NML2 with a layered structure and LMO with a perovskite structure. It is generally known that enthalpy is a parameter that quantifies the energy barrier or hindrance associated with phase formation, which reflects thermodynamic stability and feasibility of a particular phase transition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0048_R_d137282480e969" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Acta. Mater.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">In materials science research, the utilization of oxide precursors for theoretical calculations is a widespread practice due to the well-defined crystal structures and stoichiometry they offer, enabling accurate modeling of material properties. Thus, based on the Equations&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202414627-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>) and (<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202414627-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a>) for the formations of NML2 and LMO respectively, the mixed enthalpies are calculated by the Equations&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202414627-disp-0003" title="Link to equation">3</a>) and (<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202414627-disp-0004" title="Link to equation">4</a>):

         </div>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Here, “E” denotes the formation energy of each species, which can be obtained from the DFT calculation results. The energies of NML, LMO, Na<sub>2</sub>O, NiO, MnO<sub>2</sub>, and La<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> are −8.72, −11.76, −3.96, −5.39, −8.04, −12.36&nbsp;eV atom<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The calculated standard enthalpy of mixing (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/663f8031-cea8-42d4-ad76-c2475d9f0380/adfm202414627-math-0005.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202414627:adfm202414627-math-0005" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202414627-math-0005.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>H</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>m</mi>
                           <mi>i</mi>
                           <mi>x</mi>
                        </mrow>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>0</mn>
                           <mi>K</mi>
                        </mrow>
                     </msubsup>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\Delta H_{mix}^{0K}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>) values for NML2 and LMO are −1.06 and −2.21&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. These values indicate that the elements involved can distribute uniformly and maintain stability within the layered and perovskite structure lattices, as per the phase formation theory (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0003">3h</a>). Although DFT calculations suggest a slightly lower formation energy for LMO compared to NML, both values are negative and comparable, implying the spontaneous formation of both phases. To further analyze the thermodynamic stability of both layered NML2 and perovskite LMO at room temperature, their mixed Gibbs free energies (<i>ΔG</i><sub>mix</sub>) were calculated using the Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202414627-disp-0005" title="Link to equation">5</a>):

            <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202414627-disp-0005"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

                     <semantics>

                           </msub>
                           <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>

                              <mi>mix</mi>

                        </mrow>

                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(5)</span></div>where the T is 298 K (room temperature), and the mixed entropy (<i>ΔS</i><sub>mix</sub>) was calculated based on the Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202414627-disp-0006" title="Link to equation">6</a>):
