                        </mtable>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{eqnarray}&amp;&amp;\Delta {{{\mathrm{S}}}_{{\mathrm{mix}}}}\nonumber\\ &amp;&amp;= - {\mathrm{R}}\left[ {{{{\left( {\sum_{{\mathrm{a\ }} = \ 1}^{\mathrm{n}} {{{\mathrm{x}}}_{\mathrm{a}}}{\mathrm{ln}}{{{\mathrm{x}}}_{\mathrm{a}}}} \right)}}_{{\mathrm{A}} - {\mathrm{site}}}} + {{{\left( {\sum_{{\mathrm{b\ }} = \ 1}^{\mathrm{n}} {{{\mathrm{x}}}_{\mathrm{b}}}{\mathrm{ln}}{{{\mathrm{x}}}_{\mathrm{b}}}} \right)}}_{{\mathrm{B}} - {\mathrm{site}}}} + 3{{{\left( {\sum_{{\mathrm{c\ }} = \ 1}^{\mathrm{n}} {{{\mathrm{x}}}_{\mathrm{c}}}{\mathrm{ln}}{{{\mathrm{x}}}_{\mathrm{c}}}} \right)}}_{{\mathrm{O}} - {\mathrm{site}}}}} \right]\nonumber\\ \end{eqnarray}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(6)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>In the case of layered NML2, <i>x<sub>a</sub></i>, <i>x<sub>b</sub></i> and <i>x<sub>c</sub></i> are the mole amount of the elements existed in Na-, TM-, and O- site; while in the case of LMO, <i>x<sub>a</sub></i>, <i>x<sub>b</sub></i>, and <i>x<sub>c</sub></i> are the mole amount of the elements existed in A-, B-, and O-site for perovskite structure. Consequently, the <i>ΔS<sub>mix</sub></i> values of NML2 and LMO are 0.01602 and 0.01295 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup> K<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Moreover, the values of <i>ΔG<sub>mix</sub></i> at 298 K for NML2 and LMO are −5.83 and −6.07&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, demonstrating both the structures of layered NML2 and perovskite LMO are thermodynamically stable at room temperature. This implies that there is a competition between the perovskite phase and the layered phase during high-temperature calcination process. This also suggests that the perovskite LMO phase is likely to be dispersed alongside the La distribution around the particles, which will be further validated by the subsequent TEM results.</p>
         
         <p>The HRTEM images of NM and NML2 are shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0004">4a,b</a>, respectively, revealing a distinct layered structure in both samples. Their lattice fringes are consistent with the (003) plane of sodium ion-based layered oxide (JCPDS 54-0887). Notably, the lattice spacing of NML2 is measured as 0.580&nbsp;nm, which exceeds that of NM sample (0.543&nbsp;nm), aligning well with the XRD refinement outcomes. Furthermore, a continuous outer layer with a thickness of ≈2&nbsp;nm can be clearly observed in the NML2 sample (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0004">4b</a>), but absent in NM sample (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0004">4a</a>). Utilizing fast Fourier transform (FFT), the outer layer on NML2 particles is assigned to a perovskite-like phase, confirming our hypothesis of La enrichment at the particle edge.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414627-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c797f7b3-d8ee-4b82-ae6a-8fdac60462cb/adfm202414627-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c797f7b3-d8ee-4b82-ae6a-8fdac60462cb/adfm202414627-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/875bf1e6-d663-49eb-a7fd-a1e7fcde29b3/adfm202414627-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c797f7b3-d8ee-4b82-ae6a-8fdac60462cb/adfm202414627-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414627-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414627" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">TEM and corresponding structure and elements analysis for both NM and NML2: a) HRTEM image of NM (inset: diagrammatic sketch of crystal structure, FFT and TM layer measured spacing map); b) HRTEM image of NML2 samples (inset: FFT on both surface and bulk regions, crystal structure, and measured spacing of TM layers in bulk phases); EDS mapping plots of c) NM and d) NML2.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The stable perovskite surface layer is expected to improve the humidity resistance of NML materials by separating the air from the bulk layered phase. To evaluate this, we subjected NM and NML2 samples to humid air (80% humidity) for 15 days and examined their surface morphologies using SEM. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information) illustrates numerous small particle clusters on the surface of the NM sample (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a</a>, Supporting Information), which are absent in the NML2 sample (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3b</a>, Supporting Information). Our previous investigations suggest that these small particle clusters may consist of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and NaOH impurities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0036_R_d137282480e1574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nano Res.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This observation highlights the significant improvement in air stability conferred by the second perovskite LMO phase present on the surface of NML2 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0004">4b</a>). Furthermore, the energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) elemental mapping results from Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0004">4c,d</a> demonstrate uniform distribution of all elements across both NM and NML2 samples. Specifically, signal intensities reveal a higher density of La and Mn in the NML2 sample compared to Na and Ni, indicating localized concentration of La and Mn on the particle surface.</p>
         
         <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was employed to investigate the valence states of TM elements before and after La-doping. The XPS binding energies in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information) were calibrated by standard C 1s peak (set at 284.6&nbsp;eV). By analyzing the high resolution XPS spectra of La 3d, Ni 2p, and Mn 2p in both pristine NM and NML2 samples, the influence of La-doping on the valence states of Ni and Mn was illuminated. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a</a> (Supporting Information), the split high resolution XPS peak of La 3d5/2 at 834.1&nbsp;eV indicates presence of La<sup>3+</sup> in NML2 material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0049_R_d137282480e1596" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, comparative high-resolution XPS analysis of Ni 2p and Mn 2p in NM and NML2 samples reveals the reorganization of their chemical states upon La introduction (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5b,c</a>, Supporting Information). The binding energies of Ni 2p and Mn 2p in NM and NML2 samples align well with the previous literature findings.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0030_R_d137282480e1605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ACS Nano">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Significantly, compare to the pristine NM sample, the Ni 2p3/2 peak position in the NML2 sample shifts to a lower binding energy, while the Mn 2p3/2 peak shifts to a higher energy. This shift demonstrates that La-doping promotes an increase in the relative contents of Ni<sup>2+</sup> and Mn<sup>4+</sup>. Similar observations were reported by Zhang et&nbsp;al. for La-doped Na(Ni<sub>0.4</sub>Cu<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>)<sub>0.999</sub>La<sub>0.001</sub>O<sub>2</sub> sample.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0050_R_d137282480e1630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Chem. Eng. J.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The atomic ratio of Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> are estimated by comparing the area under the fitted curves in NM and NML2, and shown in Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information). Interestingly, unlike the trend observed with La-doping in layered cathode materials affecting the valence states of Ni and Mn, doping with Fe, Co or Sb in Ni/Mn based layered cathode tends to shift the valence states of Ni/Mn toward trivalence.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0030_R_d137282480e1650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ACS Nano">30</a>, <a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0051_R_d137282480e1650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Energy Stor. Mater.">51</a>, <a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0052_R_d137282480e1650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Electrochim. Acta.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This difference may be attributed to the strong metallic nature of La, which results in a stronger chemical bonding with oxygen (La─O) compared to that of Ni─O and Mn─O.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0053_R_d137282480e1655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - J. Solid State Chem.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The balance of charge and structure contributes to the differentiation in valence states between Ni and Mn. Importantly, the presence of Ni<sup>2+</sup> and Mn<sup>4+</sup> in a low-spin state weakens the Jahn–Teller effect, thereby enhancing the stability of NML cathodes during the charging/discharging process.</p>
         
         <p>Considering the limitation of XPS in providing chemical environment information within a few nanometers of the material surface, we further performed X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) characterization for NM and NML samples. This aims to enhance the reliability of analyzing the oxidation states and coordination environments of TM elements in the bulk phase. The X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) spectra of Ni and Mn K-edges for both NM and NML samples are shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0005">5a,b</a>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0005">5a</a>, Ni K-edge spectra of NM and NML show an increased energy compare to Ni(II)O, indicating Ni ions in NM and NML exist in an oxidation state ranging between +2 and +3, with a predominance of +2. Specifically, the Ni K-edge energy for NM is further higher than that of NML, suggesting a decrease in the average valence state of Ni after La doping. Similarly, changes are observed in the XANES spectra at the Mn K-edge for NM and NML samples As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0005">5b</a>, the edge regions of the Mn K-edge spectra for both NM and NML are situated between Mn(III)O and Mn(IV)O, and closer to Mn(IV)O. This positioning suggests that the oxidation states of the Mn ion in both samples are predominantly range between +3 and +4, with a tendency toward the +4 state. Moreover, the higher energy Mn K-edge observed in NML2 indicates an increase in the average valence state of Mn due to La-doping, consistent with the XPS results.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414627-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/10ea1557-8b39-42dd-a97d-c4a64012411f/adfm202414627-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/10ea1557-8b39-42dd-a97d-c4a64012411f/adfm202414627-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9915e505-0a8f-4612-a9a3-7c5a0dd73f32/adfm202414627-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/10ea1557-8b39-42dd-a97d-c4a64012411f/adfm202414627-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414627-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414627" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XANES spectra at the a) Ni K-edge and b) Mn K-edge for NM and NML2 samples (inset: local magnifications); c) the calculated pDOS results for NM and NML cathode; EXAFS spectra at the d) Ni K-edge and g) Mn K-edge for NM and NML2 samples; the WT-EXAFS spectra of e,f) Ni K-edge and h,i) Mn K-edge for NM and NML2 samples (the blue-yellow-red color gradient indicates the increase of EXAFS intensity); ELF mapping of j) NM and k) NML (the blue–green–yellow–red color gradient indicates the increased level of electron density at specific locations).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Changes in the coordination environment of TM ions significantly influence the intrinsic electronic structure of materials, impacting their electronic conductivity properties. To investigate the influence of La doping on the electronic structure of NML, we conducted DFT calculations. Total density of states (tDOS) diagrams of NM and NML samples show both exhibit favorable electrical conductivity, with La doping not causing significant alterations in their electronic structure (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). Analysis of partial Density of States (pDOS) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0005">5c</a>) reveals that the La 3d orbital is located at a higher energy position of ≈5.42&nbsp;eV (inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0005">5c</a>), indicating higher covalency in the La─O bond and La─TM bonds. Upon doping with electrochemically inert La atoms, the Mn 3d orbital of the NML shifts to a higher energy level, resulting in a reduced electron density at the Fermi level. This suggests that La doping reduces the charge transfer of high spin Mn<sup>3+</sup> ions, consistent with XPS and XANES findings. Simultaneously, stronger Mn─O and Mn─TM bonds formed, anticipated to minimize the distortion of TMO<sub>6</sub> octahedra during charge–discharge cycles, thereby stabilizing the material structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0054_R_d137282480e1717" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In the Fourier-transformed extended X-ray adsorption fine structure (FT-EXAFS) spectra shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0005">5d,g</a> for Ni and Mn K-edges in both NM and NML2 samples, two major peaks correspond to the single scattering path involving metal and adjacent oxygen atoms (TM-O<sub>6</sub>) at first coordination and interaction between metal ions (TM-TM<sub>6</sub>) on the <i>ab</i> plane at second coordination. It is evident that the intensities of Ni and Mn peaks in the La-doped NML sample are slightly lower compared to pristine NM, likely due to disordering in the concentration and symmetry of TM─O and TM─TM chemical bonds following La doping.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0030_R_d137282480e1733" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ACS Nano">30</a>, <a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0055_R_d137282480e1733" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Phys. Lett. A">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> By fitting the FT-EXAFS spectra, more structural information are obtained (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7, S8</a>, and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). The observed decrease in the average Ni─O and Mn─O bond lengths listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information) suggests enhanced hybridization within these bonds following La incorporation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0010_R_d137282480e1748" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This effect likely due to stronger electronegativity of La (1.10) compared to Ni (1.91) and Mn (1.55).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0056_R_d137282480e1754" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Nat. Commun.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to the wavelet transform (WT) spectra of Ni and Mn K-edge for NM and NML2 samples (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0005">5e,f,h,i</a>), it is observed that the scattering peak intensity of TM (Ni/Mn)-TM in NML2 sample is slightly weaker compare to that in the pristine NM sample. This is likely due to distortion in the local coordination environment of TM in <i>ab</i> plane, induced by larger radius of La ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0057_R_d137282480e1764" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Nat. Energy">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Combining XRD refinement analysis (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), we clearly demonstrate the structural evolution resulting from La doping. The larger radius of La ions induces slight distortion in the TM atom arrangement within the TM <i>ab</i> plane, leading to a more discrete structure compared to the original NM. Additionally, the strong electronegativity of La shortens the TM─O bond lengths, enhancing their binding. Consequently, the TM-O interlayer spacing decreases while the Na–O interlayer spacing increases, resulting in an overall expansion of the c-axis interlayer spacing. This adjustment improves structural stability and facilitates Na ion diffusion.</p>
         
         <p>The electron localization function (ELF) is a computational method employed to assess the degree of electron localization or delocalization within a system. In this case, the ELF analysis was performed to understand the local chemistry, orbital hybridization, and the presence of lone pairs in the NM and NML structure. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0005">5j,k</a> shows charge distribution analysis before and after La doping. Results indicate highly localized electrons around La atoms, suggesting La remains inert within the electrochemical system without participating in charge compensation. However, La doping increases electron delocalization in surrounding O atoms, enhancing anionic redox reaction (ARR) activity and facilitating charge transfer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0058_R_d137282480e1781" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Energy Environ. Sci.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This enhancement in ARR activity and Mn─O bond stability due to La doping will be confirmed in subsequent cyclic voltammetry (CV) analysis.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical properties of the prepared NM and three NML cathodes were first evaluated in the half cell system with sodium metal as the counter electrode. The desodiation/sodiation behaviors in different potential ranges (2.0–4.0&nbsp;V and 2.0–4.2&nbsp;V) of these cathodes were characterized through galvanostatic charge–discharge method. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0006">6a</a>, the pristine NM electrode exhibits typical multiple voltage plateaus in the potential range of 2.0–4.2&nbsp;V at 0.2 C, reflecting various phase transitions of O3-phase layered cathode during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction processes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0059_R_d137282480e1797" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Chem. Eng. J.">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, compared to NM electrode, the charge/discharge curves of the NML electrodes show less distinct potential plateaus. This trend is also observed in their charge/discharge profiles between 2.0 and 4.0&nbsp;V (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). Generally, frequent phase transitions can compromise material structure integrity, whereas a gradual profile suggests moderate structural evolution.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0060_R_d137282480e1806" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on the charge/discharge behaviors between 2.0 and 4.2&nbsp;V shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0006">6a</a>, La-modified electrodes exhibit higher capacities and average potentials. The reversible discharge capacities during the second cycle for NM, NML1, NML2, and NML3 are 152.3, 165.9, 176.6, and 171.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, at 0.2 C, retaining 93.6%, 97.1%, 99.9%, and 99.5% of their first discharge capacity, respectively. In addition, the average potential of the NML2 electrode (3.19&nbsp;V) surpasses that of the NM electrode (2.91&nbsp;V), indicating that appropriate La modification enhances the capacity, reversibility, and average potential of NML electrodes. Enhanced capacity and average potential in a full battery system suggest higher energy density when employing NML electrodes.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414627-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/259476be-62bb-4919-aa52-16768150c2a6/adfm202414627-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/259476be-62bb-4919-aa52-16768150c2a6/adfm202414627-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4d0f64c6-dd34-4b2f-8d2a-c7f9b0c17a58/adfm202414627-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/259476be-62bb-4919-aa52-16768150c2a6/adfm202414627-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414627-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414627" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical properties of the NM and NML samples: a) Charge–discharge curves for the initial three cycles in a potential range of 2.0–4.2&nbsp;V at 0.2 C; b) CV curves of NM and NML2 electrodes in a potential range of 2.0–4.2&nbsp;V at a scanning rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>; Rate-cycle capabilities in the potential region of c) 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V and d) 2.0–4.2&nbsp;V; Cycling performances at 1 C in the potential region of e) 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V and f) 2.0–4.2&nbsp;V; SEM images of g) NM and h) NML2 electrodes after 100 cycles.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The electrochemical redox mechanisms of NM and NML electrodes were investigated using CV at a low scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, providing insights into their behavior (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). It is generally accepted that the redox couples reflect Na<sup>+</sup>/ordering process of the vacancy in the TMO<sub>2</sub> plate, causing the multiple reversible phase transition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0061_R_d137282480e1850" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Chem. Commun.">61</a>, <a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0062_R_d137282480e1850" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Adv. Mater.">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0006">6b</a>, it is evident that compared to NM, NML2 exhibits significantly diminished redox peaks, suggesting reduced multiphase transformations. Moreover, the redox peaks shift toward higher potential, consistent with charge/discharge results. Particularly, NML3 displays stronger redox peaks above 4.0&nbsp;V and enhanced reversibility compared to NM (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting improved reversible ARR. These qualities are advantageous for enhancing the capacity of NML materials, elevating their reaction potential, and consequently increasing the energy density of SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>The rate characteristics of NM and NML electrodes were investigated across different potential ranges of 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V and 2.0–4.2&nbsp;V, respectively. Compared to pristine NM, the three NML electrodes exhibit significantly improved rate capability. Notably, NML2 demonstrates the highest rate capability, with reversible capacities in the potential range of 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V at current rates of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10 C being 135.4, 130.1, 123.8, 117.0, 110.7, 102.2, and 94.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0006">6c</a>), and in the potential range of 2.0–4.2&nbsp;V being 197.5, 181.2, 162.2, 147.8, 131.9, 104.4, and 82.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0006">6d</a>), respectively. Interestingly, capacities of all NM and NML electrodes in the potential range of 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V at high rate of 10 C exceed those at 2.0–4.2&nbsp;V. This trend aligns with our prior research findings,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0030_R_d137282480e1876" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ACS Nano">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> primarily attributed to structural degradation caused by the formation of a new O3 phase with very low Na content above 4.0&nbsp;V, particularly under high current rates.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0051_R_d137282480e1882" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Energy Stor. Mater.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This implies that controlling the charging cut-off potential is crucial for optimizing the power performance of SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Given the close relationship between structural stability and cycling performance, we further evaluated the cycle performances of NM and NML electrodes within the potential ranges of 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V and 2.0–4.2&nbsp;V at 1 C, respectively. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0006">6e,f</a>, all NML samples demonstrate superior cycling stability across both potential ranges. Notably, the NML2 electrode exhibits optimal capacity retention, retaining 96.9% in the 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V range and 84.3% in the 2.0–4.2&nbsp;V range after 100 cycles, while the NM electrodes retain only 66.3% and 23.0%, respectively. Compared to the previously reported layered cathode materials for SIBs (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), the cycling stability and rate capability of NML2 electrode in this study are outstanding.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0051_R_d137282480e1896" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Energy Stor. Mater.">51</a>, <a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0063_R_d137282480e1896" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - J. Alloys Compd.">63</a>-<a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0072" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0072_R_d137282480e1896" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 72 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">72</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on the excellent cycling stability observed at a 1 C rate within the 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V range, we conducted more rigorous cycling tests at 2 C and extended the cycle number to 500 cycles within the 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V range. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information), after 500 cycles at 2 C, the NML2 electrode retains 66.3% of its initial capacity, whereas pristine NM retains only 28.8%. This highlights the effective enhancement in cyclic ability conferred by La modification in the NML materials. To clarify the underlying mechanism of this enhanced cycling performance, we examined the morphologies of NM and NML2 electrodes after 100 cycles between 2.0 and 4.2&nbsp;V. SEM images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0006">6g,h</a>) reveal significant layer cracks in the NM sample, contrasting with the intact and robust morphology of the NML2 sample. These cracks in the NM sample likely result from repeated phase transitions, leading to capacity decay over cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0073" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0073_R_d137282480e1908" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 73 - Nano Energy">73</a>, <a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0074" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0074_R_d137282480e1908" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 74 - Nano Res.">74</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This indicates that the incorporation of La in NML samples enhances structural integrity and mitigates crack formation compared to pristine NM.</p>
         
         <p>To further understand the structural and chemical quasi-reversibility evolution of the La-modified NML electrode during electrochemical cycling, in situ XRD patterns were collected for NM and NML2 electrodes between 2.0 and 4.2&nbsp;V at 0.1 C. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0007">7a–d</a>, during the initial stages of charging, both NM and NML2 show a shift toward lower angles for the (003) and (006) diffraction peaks, accompanied by the disappearance of the (104) peak. This indicates a transition in both electrodes from the original hexagonal O3 phase to the hexagonal P3 phase, with gradual growth in interlayer spacing (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). Notably, for NM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0007">7a,b</a>), the O3 (003) peak initially shifts from 16.6° to 16.2°, forming the monoclinic O'3 phase, and further to ≈15.8°, transitioning to the hexagonal P3 phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0075" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0075_R_d137282480e1928" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 75 - Adv. Energy Mater.">75</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the modified NML2 electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0007">7c,d</a>), exhibits the absence of the O'3 (003) peak at 16.2°, indicating fewer phase transitions, consistent with the charging profiles in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0006">6a</a>. When the voltage exceeds 4&nbsp;V, the (003) peak of P3 phase shifts slightly to a higher angle, indicating the emergence of a new phase. According to Wang et&nbsp;al.,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0022_R_d137282480e1940" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> this phase is likely a new hexagonal O3 phase (denoted as O3<sub>1</sub>). Beyond 4.1&nbsp;V in the plateau region with very low sodium content below 0.4, the (003) peak at 15.8° disappears, replaced by a peak at ≈20.1°, indicating the formation of the hexagonal O3<sub>2</sub> phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0022_R_d137282480e1950" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0007">7e</a> schematically illustrates the structural evolution and lattice parameters of the NML2 sample during charging. The c-axis lattice parameters of NM and NML2 samples at different charging stages were determined through Rietveld refinements of in situ XRD patterns (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0007">7f</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information), demonstrating less structural change in the La-modified NML2 system. The enhanced structural integrity of NML2 should be attributed to stronger La─O chemical bonds and increased Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion spacing facilitated by the larger diameter of La<sup>3+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0027_R_d137282480e1969" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Alloys Compd.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414627-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c432bc15-7910-4746-b283-387b6114b99b/adfm202414627-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c432bc15-7910-4746-b283-387b6114b99b/adfm202414627-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9e0f0783-e51f-4158-aced-3abddb6fe1b4/adfm202414627-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c432bc15-7910-4746-b283-387b6114b99b/adfm202414627-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414627-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414627" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">In situ XRD and corresponding contour plots collected at the first charge/discharge process in the potential range of 2.0–4.2&nbsp;V at 0.1 C for a,b) NM and c,d) NML2; e) schematic diagram of structural and lattice parameters evolution for the NML2 sample during charging process; f) c-axis parameters of NM and NML2 samples obtained from refined in situ XRD data.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The electrical kinetic behaviors of NM and NML electrodes were further characterized using electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS). <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0008">8a,b</a> exhibits the Nyquist plots and corresponding equivalent circuits for NM and NML electrodes at open-circuit voltage (OCV) of 2.0&nbsp;V after the 3rd and 100th cycles, respectively. Each plot consists of a semicircle in the middle to high-frequency range and a sloped line below 5&nbsp;Hz. The detailed physical implications are presented in the corresponding equivalent circuit (inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0008">8a,b</a>). The close agreement between experimental data (circles) and fitted results (solid lines) indicates effective fitting based on the equivalent circuits. According to the fitted values provided in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information), the total impedance of NML electrodes is significantly lower than that of pristine NM electrodes, suggesting markedly improved electrical kinetics due to La modification. Even after 100 cycles, the impedance increase in NML electrodes remains insignificant compared to pristine NM electrodes. This enhancement is likely attributed to enhanced surface stability and accelerated Na<sup>+</sup> migration facilitated by La modification at both surface and bulk levels.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414627-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8c8592b3-9a00-400b-bd92-fc085e3ac697/adfm202414627-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8c8592b3-9a00-400b-bd92-fc085e3ac697/adfm202414627-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/113fef73-e207-4573-9f95-7c81952d487d/adfm202414627-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8c8592b3-9a00-400b-bd92-fc085e3ac697/adfm202414627-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414627-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414627" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">EIS spectra of the NM and NML electrodes at the OCV of 2.0&nbsp;V after a) three cycles and b) 100 cycles (inset: the corresponding equivalent circuit); c) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients from GITT characterizations of NM and NML2 electrodes; d) The schematic diagram of Na<sup>+</sup>-ion migration in NML sample; e) Na<sup>+</sup>-ion diffusion energy barriers in NM and NML samples; f) reversible charge/discharge curves and g) cycling performances of NM and NML2 electrodes at a low-temperature of −20&nbsp;°C in 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V at 0.1 C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To investigate the influence of La-doping on Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, we employed both Galvanostatic Intermittent Titration Technique (GITT) experiments and Nudged Elastic Band (NEB) theoretical calculation. GITT tests were performed on the NM and NML2 samples, with results shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0008">8c</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0008">8c</a> presents the logarithmic Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (D<sub>Na+</sub>) derived from the GITT tests plotted against charge–discharge cycle times. Within the 3–4&nbsp;V voltage range, the NML2 sample exhibits a D<sub>Na+</sub> value (LogD<sub>Na+</sub> of ≈6.73 m<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>) ≈0.48 orders of magnitude higher than that of the NM sample (LogD<sub>Na+</sub> of ≈6.25 m<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>).</p>
         
         <p>Using NEB calculations, we further evaluated the theoretical Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in the NM and NML samples. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0008">8d</a> illustrates three migration sites (‘1′, ‘2′, and ‘3′), where site ‘1′ represents the starting position of Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy diffusion, and site 3 denotes the final position. Specifically focusing on the pathway near the La atom, we investigated the impact of La doping on Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. In NM sample, the migration barrier for Na<sup>+</sup>-ions from site ‘1′ to site ‘2′ is 1.082&nbsp;eV, while in the NML sample, it reduces to 0.948&nbsp;eV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0008">8e</a>). This trend persists from site ‘2′ to site ‘3′. These findings indicate that La-doping effectively enhances Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, thereby improving the rate capability of NML electrodes.</p>
         
         <p>The improved electrochemical kinetics suggest that the NML2 electrode exhibits promising performance at low temperatures. Following activation at room temperature, both NM and NML2 electrodes were subjected to comparative testing at −20&nbsp;°C. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0008">8f</a>, within the potential range of 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V at 0.1 C rate and −20&nbsp;°C, both samples demonstrate discharge capacities of ≈115 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. These values are slightly lower compared to room temperature, likely due to increased polarization at the extremely low temperature. Notably, maintains smoother charge/discharge profiles compared to the NM electrode, similar to its behavior at room temperature. This indicates effective suppression of phase transitions in the La-modified sample even at −20&nbsp;°C. Further cycling for 150 cycles at −20<sup>&nbsp;</sup>°C reveals significant capacity retention, with the NML2 electrode retaining 89.2% of its initial capacity, whereas the NM electrode retains only 48.9% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0008">8g</a>). Clearly, La modification significantly improves the reversible capacity and cyclic stability of O3-type NML electrode under low-temperature conditions. The excellent performance of the NML2 electrode at low temperatures makes it enabling applications in cold climates.</p>
         
         <p>To assess the practical applicability of the La-modified O3-NML cathode material, full cells were assembled using NM and NML2 as cathode materials, paired with commercial hard carbon (HC) as the anode material. The schematic of the full cell working principle is illustrated in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0009">9a</a>. First, we examined the electrochemical performance of HC anode, as shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13–S15</a> (Supporting Information). The HC anode exhibits a capacity of 275 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C rate. Consequently, based on their respective specific capacities, the mass ratio of anode-to-cathode (N/P ratio) is ≈1.5:1. Both the cathode and anode underwent electrochemical pre-sodiation treatment before cell assembly.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0076" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0076_R_d137282480e2117" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 76 - Adv. Energy Mater.">76</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0009">9b</a> shows that the NML2||HC cell achieves a discharge capacity of 149.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 C (calculated based on the mass of cathodic active material), whereas the NM||HC cell reaches 143.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and the energy density increased from 254.3 to 281.9&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. These capacity values align closely with those observed in their half-cell systems (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). Additionally, the NML2||HC cell demonstrates superior rate performance compared to the NM||HC cell, delivering capacities of 149.5, 141.8, 136.4, 125.5, 102.3, and 80.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at rates of 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10 C, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0009">9c</a>). Moreover, higher cyclic stability of the NML2||HC cell is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414627-fig-0009">9d</a>, which maintains 85.7% after 100 cycles in the potential range of 1.5–4.0&nbsp;V at 1 C, while the NM||HC cell only maintains 37.9% after 100 cycles. The full cell tests demonstrate the promising application of NML2 cathode material for SIBs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414627-fig-0009"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cb5e6170-4a10-4dee-8b8e-1dd3b2a507d6/adfm202414627-fig-0009-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/cb5e6170-4a10-4dee-8b8e-1dd3b2a507d6/adfm202414627-fig-0009-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9693de74-5473-478e-8479-f8550af35e9d/adfm202414627-fig-0009-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cb5e6170-4a10-4dee-8b8e-1dd3b2a507d6/adfm202414627-fig-0009-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 9<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414627-fig-0009&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414627" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Full cell properties with the NM or NML2 as cathode and the HC as anode: a) schematic diagram of the working principle of full cell; b) charge–discharge curves in 1.5–4.0&nbsp;V at 0.1 C; c) rate-cycle capabilities; and d) cycling performances at 1 C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202414627-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202414627-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we propose a technique termed ‘atomic knife’, which involves the incorporation of larger radius atoms, such as lanthanum, into the lattice of the layered cathode material NM utilized in SIBs. The doping atoms induce structural strain, impeding grain growth during the calcination process. Additionally, their accumulation at particle edges promotes heterophase formation, enhancing the stability of the cathode material. Through La-modification, we observed significant improvements in the electrochemical rate and cycling performance of NML compared to the pristine sample. Specifically, the NML2 sample exhibited a capacity of 93.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C, with a remarkable capacity retention of 96.9% over 100 cycles at 1 C within the potential range of 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V. Even at a challenging low temperature of −20<sup>&nbsp;</sup>°C, the NML2 electrode retained a high capacity of 89.2% after 150 cycles. These enhancements are attributed to the multifaceted effects of the “atomic knife” technique: i) Reduction in particle size, beneficial for shortening Na<sup>+</sup>-ion and electron transport pathways, and mitigating structural stress from repeated phase transformations; ii) Protection of the layered structural material against undesired surface side reactions by a La-rich perovskite-like barrier outer layer; iii) Enhancement of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion capabilities by expanding Na-slabs. These findings provide promising opportunities for the design of high-performance layered oxide cathode materials tailored for high-energy and stable SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202414627-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202414627-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202414627-sec-0050">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202414627-sec-0060">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202414627-sec-0070">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202414627-sec-0070-title"> Materials Preparation</h4>
                  
                  <p>O3-NM, NML1, NML2, and NML3 samples were prepared by the citrate sol-gel method. Briefly, a certain stoichiometric ratio of anhydrous sodium acetate, nickel acetate tehydrate, manganese acetate tehydrate, and lanthanum nitrate hexahydrate were dissolved in deionized water. At the same time, a certain amount of anhydrous citric acid (CA) was added. The molar ratio of anhydrous citric acid and metal ions was 1.2:1, and the mixture was stirred continuously for 30&nbsp;min until completely dissolved. After drying overnight at 120&nbsp;°C, the gel was calcined at 500&nbsp;°C for 6&nbsp;h in the air first to decompose nitrate and remove part of the organic matter in the materials, then calcined at 900&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h to crystallize the materials. The O3-NM and NML cathode materials were obtained after cooling to room temperature. The prepared NM and NML samples were stored in a glove box to ensure they were not exposed to moisture, thereby guaranteeing the accuracy of subsequent testing.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202414627-sec-0080">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202414627-sec-0080-title"> Materials Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>ICP-OES (Agilent 5110) was used to measure the quantities of each element in NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5-x</sub>La<sub>x</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. Three replicates were taken for each sample, and the deviation of the results was found to be within ±1%. The ICP-OES measurements were conducted using external calibration with ICP calibration standards of Na, Ni, Mn, and La in the range of 0–10&nbsp;mg L<sup>−1</sup> (CertiPUR, Merck). The samples were dissolved in Aqua Regia overnight and then diluted with deionized water before analysis. Considering that there may be a small amount of Na loss during the calcination process, the atomic ratios were normalized to Ni+Mn (+La) = 1.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0077" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0077_R_d137282480e2217" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 77 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">77</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The porosity and BET specific surface area of the samples were measured by (ultramicro pulverizer, ASAP2020) nitrogen adsorption instrument. The crystal structure of the materials was characterized by XRD (BRUKER, D8 ADVANCE). The scanning range of Cu K<i>α</i> radiation (λ = 1.5418 A) was between 2<i>θ</i> = 10–80°, the scanning rate was 3° min<sup>−1</sup>, and the operating voltage was 40&nbsp;kV. In situ XRD were performed on an in situ electrochemical reaction cell with an X-ray transparent Be window using a Bruker D8 Advance diffractometer. In the range of 10–45°, the XRD patterns were collected at a scanning rate of 0.0177° with a time/step of 0.3 s. To obtain the detailed cell parameters information, Rietveld refinement of XRD was performed using a General Structure Analysis System (GSAS). The morphology of the samples was analyzed by field emission SEM (Hitachi S-4800) and TEM (FEI Talos F200S). The microstructure and element distribution of the samples were studied by HRTEM (FEI Talos F200S) and corresponding EDS (super-X). XPS (Thermo Scientific K-alpha) was used to analyze its valence state and binding energy.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202414627-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202414627-sec-0090-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>The electrochemical performance of O3-NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5-x</sub>La<sub>x</sub>O<sub>2</sub> layered cathode materials was evaluated in CR2032 coin cells. The cathodes were prepared by mixing active material (80 wt.%), Super-P carbon (10 wt.%), and polyvinylidene fluoride (10 wt.%) on the aluminum foil, and the mass-loading was ≈1.5&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. The HC anodes were prepared by mixing active material (92 wt.%), Super-P carbon (3 wt.%), and polyvinylidene fluoride (5 wt.%) on the aluminum foil, with a mass-loading of ≈1.1&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. The sodium metal was used as the counter and reference electrode. 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> was dissolved in EC/DMC (50:50 vol%)+2% FEC as the electrolyte. To ensure adequate electrolyte coverage, ≈200&nbsp;µL of electrolyte was added to each coin cell. Glass fiber (Merck Millipore) was used as the separator. The cells were assembled in a glovebox filled with Ar (O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O &lt; 0.1&nbsp;ppm) and tested at room temperature. Constant current charge–discharge tests and GITT tests were performed using the LAND battery tester (LAND CT, 2001A) at 30&nbsp;°C with an incubator. For the constant current charge–discharge tests, two kinds of potential ranges (2.0–4.0&nbsp;V and 2.0–4.2&nbsp;V) were adopted, where 1 C = 120&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> for 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V, and 1 C = 160&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> for 2.0–4.2&nbsp;V, respectively. For the GITT test, the cell was measured for 30&nbsp;min within a 2&nbsp;h relaxation time at a pulse current of 10&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. CV and EIS were measured using a Gamry Reference 6000 (Gamry Co., USA) electrochemical workstation. CV test was carried out at a scanning rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> in the potential range of 2–4.2&nbsp;V, and the frequency range of EIS test was 100 kHz–100 mHz.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202414627-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202414627-sec-0100-title"> Computational Details</h4>
                  
                  <p>All calculations were performed using the DFT framework and the projection augmented plane wave (PAW) method. The calculations were carried out using the Vienna ab initio simulation package,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0078" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0078_R_d137282480e2276" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 78 - Phys. Rev. B.">78</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which was implemented by Huasuan Technology Co., Ltd. The exchange correlation potential was based on the generalized gradient approximation proposed by Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof (PBE).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414627-bib-0079" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414627-bib-0079_R_d137282480e2281" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 79 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">79</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The cut-off energy of the plane wave was set to 480&nbsp;eV. In the iterative solution of the Kohn–Sham equation, the energy criterion was set to 10<sup>−5</sup>&nbsp;eV. All structures were relaxed until the atomic residual force was reduced to less than 0.02&nbsp;eV Å<sup>−1</sup>. In the calculation of formation energies, the NML stoichiometry of Na<sub>50</sub>Ni<sub>25</sub>Mn<sub>24</sub>La<sub>1</sub>O<sub>100</sub> and LMO stoichiometry of La<sub>16</sub>Mn<sub>16</sub>O<sub>48</sub> were employed. The optimized structures for NM and NML were Na<sub>25</sub>Ni<sub>12</sub>Mn<sub>13</sub>O<sub>50</sub> and Na<sub>25</sub>Ni<sub>12</sub>Mn<sub>12</sub>La<sub>1</sub>O<sub>50</sub>, respectively. They were further utilized for DOS analysis to calculate the electrical conductivity and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barrier. Additionally, the NEB method was also employed to determine the transition state of the diffusion barrier.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
